###begin article-title 0
Harmonin-b, an actin-binding scaffold protein, is involved in the adaptation of mechanoelectrical transduction by sensory hair cells
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 414 419 414 419 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 419 434 419 434 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 442 446 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 709 713 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We assessed the involvement of harmonin-b, a submembranous protein containing PDZ domains, in the mechanoelectrical transduction machinery of inner ear hair cells. Harmonin-b is located in the region of the upper insertion point of the tip link that joins adjacent stereocilia from different rows and that is believed to gate transducer channel(s) located in the region of the tip link's lower insertion point. In Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mutant mice defective for harmonin-b, step deflections of the hair bundle evoked transduction currents with altered speed and extent of adaptation. In utricular hair cells, hair bundle morphology and maximal transduction currents were similar to those observed in wild-type mice, but adaptation was faster and more complete. Cochlear outer hair cells displayed reduced maximal transduction currents, which may be the consequence of moderate structural anomalies of their hair bundles. Their adaptation was slower and displayed a variable extent. The latter was positively correlated with the magnitude of the maximal transduction current, but the cells that showed the largest currents could be either hyperadaptive or hypoadaptive. To interpret our observations, we used a theoretical description of mechanoelectrical transduction based on the gating spring theory and a motor model of adaptation. Simulations could account for the characteristics of transduction currents in wild-type and mutant hair cells, both vestibular and cochlear. They led us to conclude that harmonin-b operates as an intracellular link that limits adaptation and engages adaptation motors, a dual role consistent with the scaffolding property of the protein and its binding to both actin filaments and the tip link component cadherin-23.
###end p 1
###begin title 2

###end title 2
###begin p 3
The online version of this article (doi:10.1007/s00424-009-0711-x) contains supplementary material, which is available to authorized users.
###end p 3
###begin title 4
Keywords
###end title 4
###begin title 5
Introduction
###end title 5
###begin p 6
###xml 510 512 510 512 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">13</xref>
###xml 514 516 514 516 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">16</xref>
###xml 518 520 518 520 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">30</xref>
In the inner ear, hair cells detect deflections of their mechanosensitive hair bundle, an apical tuft of actin-filled stereocilia that are organised into rows of graded lengths. Hair bundle displacement results in a receptor potential. In response to a static stimulus, the hair cells make use of an adaptive feedback mechanism to restore their sensitivity to small stimuli. This adaptation manifests itself in the form of a decrease in the transduction current evoked by a step deflection of the hair bundle [13, 16, 30].
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 150 152 150 152 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">13</xref>
###xml 395 396 395 396 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">5</xref>
###xml 398 400 398 400 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR39">39</xref>
###xml 537 538 537 538 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">7</xref>
###xml 718 720 718 720 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR22">22</xref>
###xml 1108 1109 1108 1109 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR3">3</xref>
###xml 1388 1390 1388 1390 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>
###xml 1470 1471 1470 1471 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR4">4</xref>
###xml 1473 1475 1473 1475 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">13</xref>
###xml 1476 1478 1476 1478 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>
###xml 1480 1482 1480 1482 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">17</xref>
###xml 1521 1523 1521 1523 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1590 1592 1590 1592 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR23">23</xref>
###xml 1694 1695 1694 1695 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">7</xref>
###xml 1716 1718 1716 1718 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1831 1833 1831 1833 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1949 1951 1949 1951 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2174 2175 2174 2175 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">8</xref>
###xml 2177 2179 2177 2179 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">11</xref>
###xml 2181 2183 2181 2183 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">12</xref>
###xml 2185 2187 2185 2187 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>
###xml 2189 2191 2189 2191 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">40</xref>
###xml 424 427 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
The prevailing theory of mechanoelectrical transduction states that tension in "gating springs" controls the open probability of transducer channels [13]. There is strong evidence to suggest that the tip link, an oblique filament connecting the tip of a short stereocilium to the side of its tallest neighbour or a more compliant link in series with the tip link functions as the gating spring [5, 39]. Recent evidence from rat cochlear hair cells indicates that the transducer channel(s) are located near the lower end of the tip link [7]. It has been proposed that the gating spring is set under tension by myosin motor molecules that dynamically anchor the upper end of the tip link to the stereocilium actin core [22]. Adaptation can readily be interpreted within the framework of this motor model. An abrupt hair bundle deflection that increases gating spring tension opens transduction channels and evokes a displacement of the motors towards the base of the stereocilia. This adaptive movement of the motors is expected to relax gating spring tension, inducing reclosure of the transduction channels [3]. Because adaptation is incomplete, the existence of an elastic link anchoring the upper end of the tip link to the stereocilium actin core has also been inferred. This extent spring would prevent the motors from fully relaxing stimulus-evoked changes in gating spring tension [41]. Both the resting open probability and adaptation kinetics depend on calcium [4, 13-15, 17]. In the motor model of adaptation, Ca2+ ions may decrease the tension exerted on the tip link by myosins [23]. If the transduction channels are somewhat distant from the motor site, as a recent study suggests [7], the kinetics of Ca2+ feedback might be limited, at least for high-frequency auditory hair cells, by the diffusion and buffering of Ca2+ ions that flow into the cell through these channels. In another model that does not suffer from this limitation, Ca2+ ions may bind to the transduction channel (or to a regulatory molecule in its close proximity) to stabilise its closed state. In this case, an increase of the transduction current would directly promote channel reclosure [8, 11, 12, 15, 40].
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 371 372 371 372 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2</xref>
###xml 374 376 374 376 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR26">26</xref>
###xml 378 380 378 380 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR43">43</xref>
###xml 701 702 701 702 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">9</xref>
###xml 704 706 704 706 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">48</xref>
###xml 859 861 859 861 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">48</xref>
###xml 904 906 904 906 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 1019 1021 1019 1021 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">20</xref>
###xml 1023 1025 1023 1025 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR32">32</xref>
###xml 1210 1215 1210 1215 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 1215 1230 1215 1230 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 1279 1281 1279 1281 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">25</xref>
###xml 1201 1205 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 1238 1242 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
The biophysical properties of transduction in the inner ear have been extensively characterised, but little is known about the molecular components of the transduction machinery. However, recent studies have provided immunochemical and biochemical evidence that the tip link consists of cadherin-23 and protocadherin-15 that form its upper and lower parts, respectively [2, 26, 43]. Harmonin is a submembranous protein containing PDZ domains that is encoded by the gene responsible for Usher syndrome type 1C (USH1C), a disease characterised by congenital profound deafness and vestibular dysfunction and delayed onset retinopathy leading to blindness. Harmonin consists of three classes of isoforms [9, 48]. Harmonin-b isoforms are the largest ones, as they contain three PDZ domains, two coiled-coil domains and a proline, serine and threonine-rich domain [48]. Harmonin-b is an actin-binding protein [10] located in the region of the tip link upper insertion point in cochlear hair cells after postnatal day 5 (P5) [20, 32]. We thus assessed the role of harmonin-b in the mechanoelectrical transduction process by comparing currents evoked by mechanical stimulation of the hair bundle in wild-type mice and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mutant mice that lack harmonin-b isoforms only [25].
###end p 8
###begin title 9
Materials and methods
###end title 9
###begin p 10
Animal experiments were carried out in accordance with the Institut de la Sante et de la Recherche Medicale (INSERM) animal welfare guidelines, approved by the French Ministere de l'Agriculture, de l'Alimentation, de la Peche et de la Ruralite.
###end p 10
###begin title 11
Antibodies
###end title 11
###begin p 12
###xml 63 65 63 65 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 135 136 135 136 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 115 119 <span type="species:ncbi:9925">goat</span>
###xml 125 131 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
###xml 178 182 <span type="species:ncbi:9925">goat</span>
###xml 188 193 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 213 217 <span type="species:ncbi:9925">goat</span>
###xml 223 229 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
The H1b antibody used to detect harmonin-b has been described [10]. Secondary antibodies were Alexa 488-conjugated goat anti-rabbit Fab2 (1:500; Molecular Probe), Cy3-conjugated goat anti-mouse and Cy5-conjugated goat anti-rabbit (1:500; Amersham). Tetramethylrhodamine isothiocyanate-conjugated phalloidin (1:1000; Sigma) was used to label actin filaments.
###end p 12
###begin title 13
Whole-mount immunofluorescence
###end title 13
###begin p 14
###xml 417 419 417 419 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR37">37</xref>
###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
Mouse inner ears were dissected from temporal bones at different developmental stages. The cochlear shell was pierced at its apex; the round and oval windows were opened. Inner ears were then immersed in a 4% paraformaldehyde/phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) solution for 1 h at room temperature. After several washes in PBS, the cochlear shell was finely dissected, and the organ of Corti was processed as described [37]. Fluorescence images were obtained with a confocal microscope (Zeiss LSM 510 META) equipped with a Plan Apochromat 63x/1,4 oil immersion objective. Z-stack images were deconvoluted and reconstructed with the Huygens (Scientific Volume Imaging, Hilversum, The Netherlands) and Image J (Rasband WS, US NIH, Bethesda, MA, USA, , 1997-2006) softwares, respectively.
###end p 14
###begin title 15
Scanning electron microscopy
###end title 15
###begin p 16
###xml 33 38 33 38 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 38 53 38 53 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 54 58 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
Inner ears from P7 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mice were removed, pierced at the apex of the cochlea and in the round and oval windows and fixed by immersion in 2.5% glutaraldehyde in 0.1 M phosphate buffer (pH 7.3) for 2 h at room temperature. After three washes with the buffer alone, the inner ears were dissected to give direct access to the organ of Corti or the utricle. The samples were then dehydrated by successive washes in ethanol (50%, 70%, 80%, 90% and 100%), critical point dried, mounted on a stub, sputter coated with gold-palladium and examined under a Jeol JSM 6700F scanning microscope.
###end p 16
###begin p 17
Angular deviations were measured between the expected and effective positions of the kinocilia at the apical surfaces of hair cells from the cochlear apical turn, using the graphic tools of the Jeol JSM6700F software. Briefly, for each hair cell, the kinociliary deviation was determined by the angle formed by two crossing lines. The first line was drawn mediolaterally along the symmetry/planar cell polarity axis of the cell, thereby running through the expected position of the kinocilium. The second line was traced between the centre of the hair cell surface and the effective position of the kinocilium. Data were analysed using Excel (Microsoft).
###end p 17
###begin title 18
Electrophysiological recordings with a rigid glass rod
###end title 18
###begin p 19
###xml 127 132 127 132 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 132 147 132 147 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 198 199 198 199 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 255 256 255 256 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 276 278 276 278 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">36</xref>
###xml 795 796 795 796 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 808 809 808 809 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 811 812 811 812 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 856 857 856 857 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 883 884 883 884 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 1009 1010 1009 1010 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1062 1063 1062 1063 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 148 152 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
Electrophysiological recordings and hair bundle stimulations were performed in cochlear and utricular explants from C57BL6 and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mice aged between P1 and P7 (cochlear hair cells, n = 381) or between E17.5 and P1 (vestibular hair cells, n = 63) as reported [36]. Briefly, the cochlear coils or utricles were finely dissected, placed under two nylon meshes and observed under a x40 water-immersion Olympus objective mounted on an Axioscope Zeiss microscope. We recorded from cochlear outer hair cells (OHCs) that were located at a distance of approximately 50% of the total length of the cochlea, except for P7 hair cells (35% of the length of the cochlea from the apex). Extracellular and dissecting solutions were identical and composed of 146 mM NaCl, 5.8 mM KCl, 1.5 mM CaCl2, 0.7 mM NaH2PO4, 2 mM Na-pyruvate, 10 mM glucose and 10 mM N-2-hydroxyethylpiperazine-N-2-ethanesulfonic acid (HEPES; pH = 7.4, 305 mosmol/kg). Intracellular solution contained 130 mM KCl, 10 mM NaCl, 3.5 mM MgCl2, 1 mM ethyleneglycoltetraacetic acid (EGTA), 5 mM K2ATP, 0.5 mM GTP and 5 mM HEPES (pH = 7.3, 290 mosmol/kg).
###end p 19
###begin p 20
Borosilicate patch pipettes (2-3 Mohm) were approached parallel to the hair cell rows through a hole in the reticular lamina. During this step, extracellular solution was abundantly perfused to avoid contact between EGTA and the transduction apparatus which is sensitive to calcium chelators. Hair cells were whole-cell voltage clamped at room temperature (20-25degreesC) at -80 mV using an EPC-9 patch clamp amplifier and the Patchmaster software (HEKA, Lambrecht, Germany). No correction was made for liquid junction potential. Series resistance was always below 10 Mohm and was compensated to 70%. Hair cells of the vestibular utricle (UHCs) were patched at P1. Data were sampled at 100 kHz and filtered at 10 kHz (8-pole Bessel). Each transducer current presented in the figures is the average response of ten consecutive stimulations.
###end p 20
###begin p 21
###xml 812 814 798 800 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>
Each hair bundle was mechanically stimulated by applying axial step displacements with a rigid glass rod that had been fire-polished prior to the experiment to yield a tip diameter of 2-3 microm. The probe was systematically positioned against the top of the hair bundle in the bundle's plane of bilateral symmetry towards the tallest row of stereocilia at an angle of ~30degrees relative to the cell apical surface. The probe was secured to a stack-type piezoelectric actuator (PA8/12, Piezosystem Jena, Jena, Germany) driven by a low-voltage power supply (12V40, Piezosystem Jena). To limit resonance of the actuator, a voltage ramp lasting 300 micros was applied at the onset and offset of each voltage step command to the piezo-driver. As measured offline with a displacement monitor containing photodiodes [46], the first 2 ms of the time course of probe motion were well described by an exponential rise with a time constant of 490 micros. The stimulus onset was fast compared to adaptation kinetics in UHCs but relatively slow when compared to adaptation in hair bundles of the OHCs.
###end p 21
###begin title 22
Electrophysiological recordings with a fluid jet
###end title 22
###begin p 23
###xml 17 22 17 22 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 22 25 22 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;/&#8722;</italic>
###xml 22 25 22 25 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>&#8722;/&#8722;</italic></sup>
###xml 319 321 319 321 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">30</xref>
In wild-type and Ush1c-/- OHCs from the apical coil, mechanoelectrical transducer currents were also elicited using fluid jet stimulation (45 Hz sinewaves filtered at 1 kHz, 8-pole Bessel) and recorded under whole-cell voltage clamp with an Optopatch (Cairn Research, Faversham, UK) patch clamp amplifier as described [30]. The driver voltage to the fluid jet was fixed at 40 V, which elicits large, near-saturating transducer currents in control cells. Patch pipettes (resistance in the bath, 2-3 MOmega) were pulled from soda glass capillaries and coated with wax. Data were filtered at 2.5-3.0 kHz and sampled at 5 kHz using a Power 1401 data acquisition interface and Signal 3 software (CED, Cambridge, UK).
###end p 23
###begin p 24
###xml 171 172 171 172 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 185 186 185 186 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 198 199 198 199 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 201 202 201 202 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 229 230 229 230 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</sc>
###xml 482 483 482 483 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 512 513 512 513 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 540 541 540 541 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
In the case of fluid jet stimulation of the hair bundle, experimental conditions were the following. Extracellular solution contained: 135 mM NaCl, 5.8 mM KCl, 1.3 mM CaCl2, 0.9 mM MgCl2, 0.7 mM NaH2PO4, 2 mM Na-pyruvate, 5.6 mM d-glucose and 10 mM HEPES; amino acids and vitamins for Eagle's minimum essential medium (MEM) were added from concentrates (Invitrogen); pH was adjusted to 7.5; osmolality was about 308 mosmol/kg. Intracellular solution contained: 137 mM CsCl, 10 mM Na2 phosphocreatine, 2.5 mM MgCl2, 1 mM EGTA-CsOH, 2.5 mM Na2-ATP and 5 mM HEPES; pH was adjusted to 7.3 with 1 M CsOH; osmolality was about 295 mosmol/kg.
###end p 24
###begin title 25
FM1-43 dye labelling
###end title 25
###begin p 26
###xml 94 99 94 99 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 99 102 99 102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;/&#8722;</italic>
###xml 99 102 99 102 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>&#8722;/&#8722;</italic></sup>
###xml 152 154 152 154 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR18">18</xref>
###xml 103 107 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
FM1-43 dye loading experiments of hair cells in cochlear explants from neonatal wild-type and Ush1c-/- mice were performed as described by Gale et al. [18].
###end p 26
###begin title 27
Data analysis
###end title 27
###begin p 28
###xml 76 77 76 77 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 77 78 77 78 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 79 80 79 80 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 140 141 140 141 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">6</xref>
###xml 143 145 143 145 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">29</xref>
###xml 147 149 147 149 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">36</xref>
###xml 296 297 296 297 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 297 298 297 298 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 376 377 376 377 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">I</italic>
###xml 400 401 400 401 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 457 458 457 458 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 458 459 458 459 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0</sub>
###xml 589 590 589 590 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 590 591 590 591 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</sub>
###xml 596 597 596 597 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 597 598 597 598 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</sub>
###xml 620 623 620 621 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#964;</italic>
###xml 623 624 621 622 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</sub>
###xml 629 632 627 628 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#964;</italic>
###xml 632 633 628 629 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</sub>
###xml 653 654 649 650 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">A</italic>
###xml 654 656 650 652 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SS</sub>
###xml 777 780 773 774 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#964;</italic>
###xml 780 781 774 775 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</sub>
###xml 788 791 782 783 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#964;</italic>
###xml 791 792 783 784 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</sub>
###xml 870 872 862 864 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">27</xref>
Data were analysed using the Igor Pro software (Wavemetrics, Portland, OR). Po(X) curves were fitted with a three-state Boltzmann relation [6, 29, 36]. For sensitivity measurements, the mean value of the three-state Boltzmann relation derivative was calculated for displacements corresponding to Po values between 0.2 and 0.8. The adaptive decline of the transduction current I as a function of time t was fitted by the double exponential relation . There, t0 is the time at which the stimulus was applied, the fast and slow components of adaptation are characterised by their magnitudes, AF and AS, and time constants, tauF and tauS, respectively, and ASS describes the transduction current at steady state. From the fit, we thus deduced the fast and slow adaptation rates, 1/tauF and 1/tauS, and proportions,  and , respectively, as well as the extent of adaptation  [27]. Only cells that had transduction currents with a maximal amplitude over 100 pA were considered for analysis of the kinetics and extent of adaptation.
###end p 28
###begin p 29
###xml 293 294 293 294 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 447 448 447 448 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig5" ref-type="fig">5</xref>
###xml 636 637 636 637 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 647 648 647 648 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 657 658 657 658 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 670 671 670 671 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
Statistical significance was tested by using either one-way analysis of variance (one-way ANOVA) coupled to the Tukey honestly significant difference post-test using the Igor Pro software, two-way analysis of variance (two-way ANOVA) coupled to the Bonferroni post-test or two-tailed unpaired t test with Welch's correction using Prism software (GraphPad, La Jolla, CA). For variance comparison, we used the Fisher-Snedecor statistical test (Fig. 5). For correlation tests, we used the Pearson two-tailed correlation test. Statistical significances are indicated on the figures; (ns), (*), (**) and (***) correspond to non-significant (p > 0.05), p < 0.05, p < 0.01 and p < 0.001, respectively.
###end p 29
###begin title 30
Simulations
###end title 30
###begin p 31
###xml 354 356 354 356 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 460 462 460 462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 464 466 464 466 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">35</xref>
###xml 468 470 468 470 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>
###xml 517 518 517 518 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 542 543 542 543 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 543 544 543 544 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
We used a physical description of transduction that takes into consideration three elements only: an intrinsic force-displacement relation for the hair bundle that contains a nonlinear region of reduced slope as the result of gating compliance, adaptation motors that exert an active force against gating springs, and electromechanical feedback of the Ca2+-ion component of the transduction current on the force that the adaptation motor can produce at stall [34, 35, 46]. The dynamic interplay between the positions X of the hair bundle and Xa of the adaptation motor is described by two coupled equations:
###end p 31
###begin p 32
###xml 19 20 19 20 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 214 220 214 216 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#948;X</italic>
###xml 271 277 267 269 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#948;x</italic>
###xml 367 368 359 360 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 456 457 448 449 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 457 458 449 450 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 511 512 503 504 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 565 566 557 558 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 696 698 688 690 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR28">28</xref>
###xml 722 723 714 715 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 723 724 715 716 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
The external force F is applied at the hair bundle's top along the horizontal axis of a hair bundle's vertical plane of bilateral symmetry. All variables are here expressed along this axis. Hair bundle deflections deltaX are related to changes in gating spring extension deltax by a geometrical gain . In the absence of gating springs, the hair bundle is at position X = 0. The displacements of the adaptation motor are measured with respect to a position Xa = 0 for which the gating springs bear no tension if X = 0 and the channels are all closed. We assume that N identical transduction elements, each comprising a gating spring attached to a single transduction channel, operate in parallel [28]. The open probability Po of the transduction channels is a sigmoidal function of the relative position between the hair bundle and the adaptation motor:
###end p 32
###begin p 33
###xml 56 60 52 56 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">G&#8201;=&#8201;</italic>
###xml 64 65 60 61 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</sub>
###xml 142 143 138 139 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">k</italic>
###xml 143 144 139 140 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">B</sub>
###xml 175 176 171 172 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T</italic>
Here,  accounts for an intrinsic energy difference DeltaG = 10 kBT between the open and the closed states of a transduction channel, in which kB is the Boltzmann constant and T the temperature.
###end p 33
###begin p 34
###xml 9 10 9 10 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Equ1" ref-type="">1</xref>
###xml 73 74 73 74 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 109 111 109 111 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F.</italic>
###xml 207 213 207 208 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#955;</italic>
###xml 232 234 227 229 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dX</italic>
###xml 235 237 230 232 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dt</italic>
###xml 244 245 239 240 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 300 301 295 296 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 301 303 296 298 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SP</sub>
###xml 303 304 298 299 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 394 395 389 390 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 395 397 390 392 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">GS</sub>
###xml 402 403 397 398 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 403 405 398 400 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SP</sub>
###xml 567 568 562 563 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</italic>
###xml 611 622 606 613 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D&#8201;=&#8201;d/&#947;</italic>
###xml 658 659 649 650 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Equ2" ref-type="">2</xref>
###xml 763 769 754 755 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#955;</italic>
###xml 769 770 755 756 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 806 807 792 793 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 807 808 793 794 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 895 896 881 882 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 896 898 882 884 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ES</sub>
###xml 1037 1039 1023 1025 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>
###xml 1049 1050 1035 1036 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Equ2" ref-type="">2</xref>
###xml 1052 1053 1038 1039 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 1053 1055 1039 1041 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ES</sub>
###xml 1072 1073 1058 1059 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 1073 1074 1059 1060 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 1170 1172 1156 1158 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1179 1181 1165 1167 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">16</xref>
###xml 1258 1260 1244 1246 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1334 1336 1320 1322 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1405 1407 1391 1393 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1484 1486 1470 1472 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1608 1610 1594 1596 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>
Equation 1 describes the dynamical behaviour of the hair bundle position X in response to the external force F. The hair bundle moves at a velocity that is inversely proportional to the friction coefficient lambda. At steady state (dX/dt = 0), F is balanced by the sum of the elastic restoring force KSPX provided by the stereociliary pivots and the total tension  in the gating springs. Here, KGS and KSP correspond to the stiffness of the gating springs and of the stereociliary pivots, respectively. Channel opening decreases gating spring extension by a distance d that amounts to a horizontal displacement D = d/gamma at the top of the bundle. Equation 2 describes the dynamical behaviour of the adaptation motor by a linear force-velocity relation of slope lambdaa. At stall , the active motor force Fa is balanced by the combined tension in gating springs and extent springs of stiffness KES. The extent springs anchor the transduction apparatus to the stereociliary cytoskeleton and account for the incompleteness of adaptation [41]. In Eq. 2, XES is the value of Xa for which the extent springs bear no tension. To account for the regulation of adaptation by Ca2+ ions [16], we assume that the force produced by the motors at stall depends on the Ca2+ concentration at the motor site. Under the simplifying assumption that Ca2+ diffusion to this site and that binding and unbinding kinetics of Ca2+ ions to the motors are fast compared to the timescale of adaptation, this Ca2+ feedback is effectively instantaneous and approximately determined by the open probability of the transduction channels [46].
###end p 34
###begin p 35
###xml 7 8 7 8 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Equ4" ref-type="">4</xref>
###xml 38 39 38 39 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 66 68 66 68 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 102 103 102 103 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 103 106 103 106 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 177 178 177 178 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 178 179 178 179 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 179 182 179 182 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8201;=&#8201;</italic>
###xml 225 227 225 227 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 368 369 368 369 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 369 371 369 371 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">GS</sub>
###xml 376 377 376 377 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 377 379 377 379 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ES</sub>
###xml 405 411 405 407 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N&#947;</italic>
###xml 411 412 407 408 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</italic>
###xml 411 412 407 408 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>2</italic></sup>
###xml 422 428 418 419 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#955;</italic>
###xml 428 429 419 420 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 434 435 425 426 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 435 438 426 429 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 459 460 450 451 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 460 461 451 452 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 461 466 452 453 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#947;</italic>
###xml 466 467 453 454 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</italic>
###xml 466 467 453 454 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>2</italic></sup>
###xml 475 476 462 463 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 476 477 463 464 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</sub>
###xml 563 564 550 551 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 591 593 578 580 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
In Eq. 4, the dimensionless parameter S defines the strength of Ca2+ feedback on the motor force, and Fmax is the force produced by the motors when the channels are all closed (Po = 0). This equation ensures that increased Ca2+ levels at the motor site reduce active force generation and thus result in lower open probabilities of the transduction channels. Note that KGS and KES are each proportional to Ngamma2, whereas lambdaa and Fmax are proportional to Nagamma2, where Na is the total number of adaptation motors and is thus also proportional to the number N of transduction elements [34].
###end p 35
###begin p 36
###xml 192 193 192 193 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 193 194 193 194 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</sub>
###xml 259 260 255 256 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</italic>
###xml 261 262 257 258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 262 263 258 259 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">F</sub>
###xml 308 309 300 301 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 309 311 301 303 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SP</sub>
###xml 425 431 417 418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#955;</italic>
###xml 691 692 678 679 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 692 694 679 681 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SP</sub>
###xml 768 774 755 756 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#955;</italic>
###xml 779 780 761 762 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">K</italic>
###xml 780 782 762 764 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SP</sub>
We assumed in simulations that an external force of the form  was applied to the hair bundle. The response of the combined system of a hair bundle and its attached stimulus fibre of stiffness KF to a movement Delta of the fibre's base was computed by setting F=KFDelta. For numerical calculations, parameter KSP in turn corresponded to the summed stiffness of the stereociliary pivots and fibre, and the friction coefficient lambda includes the friction coefficient of the fibre and of the hair bundle. To mimic the displacement clamp conditions that were used in experiments, the fibre used in simulations had to be rigid compared to the hair bundle stiffness, resulting in large values of KSP. The rise time of hair bundle deflections was controlled by the ratio of lambda and KSP.
###end p 36
###begin p 37
Simulations were performed in Matlab, version 7.0 (The MathWorks, Natick, MA). Calculated transduction responses were fitted with a double exponential curve to extract fast and slow adaptation rates, the proportion of fast and slow components of adaptation and the adaptation extent.
###end p 37
###begin title 38
Results
###end title 38
###begin title 39
Harmonin-b relocalises to the region of the tip link upper insertion point in developing utricular hair cells and cochlear outer hair cells
###end title 39
###begin p 40
###xml 292 297 292 297 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 297 312 297 312 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 324 325 324 325 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>
###xml 496 498 496 498 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a</xref>
###xml 884 886 884 886 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1b</xref>
###xml 1385 1390 1385 1390 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a, b</xref>
###xml 1393 1399 1393 1399 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;1</label>
###xml 1473 1478 1473 1478 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 1478 1493 1478 1493 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 1500 1501 1500 1501 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 1541 1546 1541 1546 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">green</italic>
###xml 1636 1638 1636 1638 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">WT</italic>
###xml 1752 1757 1752 1757 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 1757 1772 1757 1772 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 1811 1814 1811 1814 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">red</italic>
###xml 1816 1817 1816 1817 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</bold>
###xml 1857 1862 1857 1862 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">green</italic>
###xml 1944 1946 1944 1946 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">WT</italic>
###xml 2058 2063 2058 2063 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 2063 2078 2063 2078 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
###xml 2117 2120 2117 2120 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">red</italic>
###xml 1399 2143 1399 2139 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="41">Distribution of harmonin-b in UHC and OHC hair bundles from wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> mice. <bold>a</bold> UHCs. ###xml 1399 2143 1399 2139 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="41">Distribution of harmonin-b in UHC and OHC hair bundles from wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> mice. <bold>a</bold> UHCs. ###xml 2143 2143 2139 2139 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_711_Fig1_HTML" id="MO1"/>
###xml 1393 2143 1393 2139 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig1"><label>Fig.&#160;1</label><caption><p textid="41">Distribution of harmonin-b in UHC and OHC hair bundles from wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> mice. <bold>a</bold> UHCs. A punctate harmonin-b labelling (<italic>green</italic>) can be seen in the apical region of the stereocilia in E16, E18 and P21 wild-type UHCs (<italic>WT</italic>). Actin filaments are labelled in <italic>red</italic>. <bold>b</bold> OHCs. A punctate harmonin-b labelling (<italic>green</italic>) can be seen in all stereocilia from the tall and middle rows in wild-type OHCs (<italic>WT</italic>). The labelling is located at the stereocilia tip at P3 and below the tip at P5 and P21. It is not detected in <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> OHCs. Actin filaments are labelled in <italic>red</italic>. Scale bars&#8201;=&#8201;1&#8201;&#181;m</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_711_Fig1_HTML" id="MO1"/></fig>
###xml 313 317 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 507 511 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 1494 1498 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We analysed the distribution of harmonin-b within the hair bundles of the outer hair cells of the cochlea and hair cells of the vestibular utricle. An intense harmonin-b immunostaining restricted to the apical region of the hair bundle was observed in both UHCs and OHCs, which was absent in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mice (Fig. 1). In embryonic day 16 (E16) UHCs, harmonin-b immunoreactivity was found at the very tips of stereocilia, whereas at E18, it was detected below the stereocilia tips (Fig. 1a). In P3 mice, OHCs displayed a punctate harmonin-b staining at the tip of each of the stereocilia that compose the tall and middle rows, whereas stereocilia from the small row were unlabelled. Two days later, the labelling in the tall and middle rows was no longer detected at the stereocilia tips but was present farther down in the region of the tip link upper insertion point (Fig. 1b). No labelling was detected in OHC stereocilia from the small row, which do not have a tip link insertion point on their lateral wall. These results argue in favour of the relocalization of harmonin-b from the stereocilia tip to the region of the tip link upper insertion point between E16 and E18 in the UHCs and between P3 and P5 in the OHCs. The harmonin-b labelling pattern persisted as an intense punctate staining of the tip link upper end region in both UHCs and OHCs at mature stages (Fig. 1a, b). Fig. 1Distribution of harmonin-b in UHC and OHC hair bundles from wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mice. a UHCs. A punctate harmonin-b labelling (green) can be seen in the apical region of the stereocilia in E16, E18 and P21 wild-type UHCs (WT). The labelling is located at the stereocilia tip at E16 and below the tip at E18 and P21. It is not detected in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. Actin filaments are labelled in red. b OHCs. A punctate harmonin-b labelling (green) can be seen in all stereocilia from the tall and middle rows in wild-type OHCs (WT). The labelling is located at the stereocilia tip at P3 and below the tip at P5 and P21. It is not detected in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs. Actin filaments are labelled in red. Scale bars = 1 microm
###end p 40

###xml 46 50 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 116 122 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">murine</span>
###xml 2203 2207 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 2531 2535 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 3215 3219 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 3555 3559 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We studied transduction currents in wild-type mice during the period of hair bundle maturation in UHCs and OHCs. In murine UHCs, transduction onset occurs at embryonic day 17 (E17) and is already associated with fast and slow components of adaptation [19]. We analysed how transduction features evolve between E18 and P1. The mean maximum amplitude of transduction currents increased by a factor of 2.5, from 125 +/- 26 to 320 +/- 25 pA (one-way ANOVA, p < 10-5), between E18 and P1 (Fig. 2a). By contrast, the relationship Po(X) between open probability of the transduction channels (Po) and hair bundle displacement (X) changed very little over this period. Indeed, the averaged sensitivity of the transduction current to hair bundle displacement, defined as the mean slope of the Po(X) curve between Po = 0.2 and Po = 0.8, did not vary (from 0.91 +/- 0.12 mum-1 at E18 to 1.07 +/- 0.16 mum-1 at P1; unpaired t test, p = 0.43). Only the displacement X0.5 required for the opening of 50% of the transduction channels decreased by 23%, from 644 +/- 64 nm at E18 to 480 +/- 40 nm at P1 (unpaired t test, p < 0.05; Fig. 2a). Adaptation can be characterised in terms of its extent and kinetics. The extent of adaptation (see "Materials and methods") did not change significantly between E18 and P1, regardless of the open probability chosen to make the comparison (two-way ANOVA, p = 0.06; Fig. 2a). From E18 onward, utricular transduction currents displayed an adaptive decline in two sequential phases, a rapid decline in the millisecond range, followed by a slow decline in the 5 to 100 ms range, as previously reported [45]. The rates of fast and slow components of adaptation (see "Materials and methods") remained unchanged between E18 and P1 (two-way ANOVA,  for both adaptation rates; Fig. 2a). The only significant modification observed concerned the relative balance between the fast and slow components, with the slow component of adaptation predominating at P1 but not at E18 (two-way ANOVA, p < 0.05). Left Examples of transduction current recordings in UHCs voltage clamped at -80 mV from E18 and P1 wild-type mice while applying different displacement steps with a glass probe in the excitatory direction and one 150 nm step in the inhibitory direction (calibrated voltage command of the stimulator at the top left). Right Mean maximum transduction currents recorded in E18 and P1 wild-type UHCs and Po(X) plots for E18 and P1 wild-type mice. The E18 and P1 curves were fitted with a normalised three-state Boltzmann relation: , where delta0 = 474 nm, delta1 = 117 nm, X0 = 500 nm and X1 = 353 nm for E18 UHCs, and delta0 = 555 nm, delta1 = 109 nm, X0 = 213 nm and X1 = 313 nm for P1 UHCs. Lower panels Extent of adaptation, contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation), fast adaptation rates and slow adaptation rates plotted as a function of Po for E18 and P1 wild-type UHCs. *p < 0.05, **p < 0.01 statistical significance and ns p > 0.05 non-significance. bUpper panels. Left Examples of transduction current recordings in OHCs voltage clamped at -80 mV from P1, P3 and P5 wild-type mice while applying different displacement steps with a glass probe in the excitatory direction and a 150 nm step in the inhibitory direction (calibrated voltage command of the stimulator at the top left). Right Mean maximum transduction currents recorded between P1 and P7 in wild-type OHCs and Po(X) plots for P1, P3, P5 and P7 wild-type mice. The P1 and P7 curves were fitted with a three-state Boltzmann relation: , where delta0 = 192 nm, delta1 = 67 nm, X0 = 255 nm and X1 = 176 nm for P1 OHCs and delta0 = 159 nm, delta1 = 63 nm, X0 = 47 nm and X1 = 173 nm for P7 OHCs. Lower panels Extent of adaptation, contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation), fast adaptation rates and slow adaptation rates plotted as a function of Po for P1, P3, P5 and P7 wild-type OHCs
###end p 43

###xml 619 620 615 616 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 625 628 621 624 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;12</sup>
###xml 655 657 651 653 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2b</xref>
###xml 838 840 831 833 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;1</sup>
###xml 867 869 857 859 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;1</sup>
###xml 886 887 876 877 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 894 895 884 885 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 900 902 890 892 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;2</sup>
###xml 911 912 901 902 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">X</italic>
###xml 912 915 902 905 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0.5</sub>
###xml 1002 1003 988 989 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 1010 1011 996 997 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 1016 1018 1002 1004 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;4</sup>

###xml 1140 1141 1126 1127 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 1146 1148 1132 1134 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;4</sup>

###xml 1212 1213 1198 1199 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>

###xml 1580 1582 1566 1568 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">44</xref>
###xml 1584 1586 1570 1572 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR49">49</xref>
###xml 1795 1796 1781 1782 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 1796 1797 1782 1783 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 1820 1821 1806 1807 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 1826 1828 1812 1814 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;4</sup>
###xml 1861 1863 1847 1849 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2b</xref>
###xml 1911 1912 1897 1898 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 1912 1913 1898 1899 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 1961 1962 1947 1948 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 1962 1963 1948 1949 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 2141 2143 2127 2129 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2b</xref>
###xml 2348 2350 2334 2336 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR49">49</xref>
###xml 2435 2437 2421 2423 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR33">33</xref>
###xml 110 113 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 267 272 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 347 351 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 2203 2208 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 2322 2325 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 2420 2425 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
A previous study has described the time course for the acquisition and maturation of transduction currents in rat cochlear OHCs maintained in organotypic cultures [49]. We thought it necessary to verify whether a similar maturation of transduction currents occurs in mouse OHCs. We thus recorded from acute preparations of the organ of Corti from mice aged between P1 and P7 (Fig. 2b). All the transduction characteristics in OHCs displayed significant maturation between these two stages. The mean maximal amplitude of transduction currents increased by a factor of 7, from 142 +/- 50 to 968 +/- 58 pA (one-way ANOVA, p < 10-12), between P1 and P7 (Fig. 2b). The sensitivity of the transduction current to hair bundle displacement also increased over this period. We found that averaged sensitivity almost doubled, from 1.86 +/- 0.31 mum-1 at P1 to 3.29 +/- 0.29 mum-1 at P7 (unpaired t test, p < 10-2), while X0.5 decreased by a factor of 2, from 314 +/- 34 nm at P1 to 165 +/- 12 nm at P7 (unpaired t test, p < 10-4; Fig. 2b). Adaptation also varied between P1 and P7. The extent of adaptation increased over this period (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4), more markedly for intermediate open probabilities than near Po = 0 or 1 (Fig. 2b). Thus, although the extent of adaptation at P1 decreased almost linearly as a function of Po, it was almost invariant at P7 for Po < 0.5. The OHC transduction currents displayed two sequential phases of adaptive decline: a fast phase in the submillisecond range, followed by a slow phase, in the 5 to 100 ms range, as previously reported [27, 29, 44, 49]. Fast and slow adaptation rates increased between P1 and P7. Increases by a factor of almost 10 for the fast adaptation rate and of almost 4 for the slow adaptation rate were observed between P1 and P7, for Po = 0.5 (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4 for both adaptation rates; Fig. 2b). Adaptation speeds, which were independent of Po until P3, subsequently became faster for small Po. Notably, the maximal transduction current, as well as the extent and speed of adaptation, underwent greater changes between P3 and P5 than between P1 and P3 or P5 and P7 (Fig. 2b). We conclude that the transduction currents recorded from mouse acute preparations of the organ of Corti reproduce the stepwise functional maturation which has been observed in rat organotypic cultures [49]. Our findings are also in agreement with those recently obtained on mouse OHCs by [33].
###end p 45
###begin title 46
###xml 51 56 51 56 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 56 71 56 71 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>
Adaptation is more complete and slightly faster in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs
###end title 46

<italic>I</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;1/[1&#8201;+&#8201;{exp(&#8722;(<italic>X</italic>&#8201;&#8722;&#8201;<italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>)/<italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>)}{1&#8201;+&#8201;exp(<italic>&#8722;</italic>(<italic>X</italic>&#160;&#8722;&#8201;<italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>)/<italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>)}], where <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;555&#160;nm, <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;109&#160;nm, <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;213&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;313&#160;nm for P1 wild-type UHCs and <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;385&#160;nm, <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;108&#160;nm, <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;558&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;229&#160;nm for P1 <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs. <bold>d</bold> Extent of adaptation curves plotted as a function of <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub> for P1 wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs. <bold>e</bold> Contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation) plotted as a function of <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub> for P1 wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs. <bold>f</bold> Fast adaptation rates and slow adaptation rates, plotted as a function of <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub> for P1 wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_711_Fig4_HTML" id="MO4"/></fig>
###xml 1447 1451 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 1565 1569 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 1690 1694 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We compared the characteristics of transduction currents in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J and wild-type UHCs at P1. Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHC hair bundles display no detectable morphological abnormalities at this stage (Fig. 3). Many of the transduction properties were unaffected in the mutant UHCs. In particular, the amplitudes of the transduction current were similar to those for the wild type (one-way ANOVA, p > 0.05; Fig. 4b). In addition, no change in averaged sensitivity or X0.5 could be detected in the mutant UHCs (unpaired t test, p = 0.11 and p = 0.07, respectively; Fig. 4c). Accordingly, the Po(X) curves of Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J and wild-type UHCs could be superimposed. Yet, the extent of adaptation was significantly greater in these cells, resulting in a residual current one third lower than in wild-type UHCs (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4; Fig. 4d). Fast and slow components of adaptation made similar contributions to the extent of adaptation in both wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs (two-way ANOVA on fast to slow adaptation ratios, p = 0.06; Fig. 4e). The fast adaptation rate tended to be greater for all Po in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J than in wild-type UHCs (Fig. 4f), but this difference was not statistically significant (two-way ANOVA, p = 0.11). However, the slow adaptation rate was significantly greater in the mutant UHCs (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4; Fig. 4f). Fig. 3Hair bundle morphology in wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J mice. Top row Scanning electron microphotographs of utricular hair bundles from wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J P1 mice. Middle and lower rows Scanning electron microphotographs of organs of Corti from wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J P7 mice and detail of an OHC hair bundle (white frames in middle row). Scale bars = 1 micromFig. 4Mechanoelectrical transduction current recordings in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. a Examples of transduction current recordings in P1 wild-type (left panel) and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs (right panel) voltage clamped at -80 mV while applying different displacement steps with a glass probe in the excitatory direction and a 150-nm step in the inhibitory direction (calibrated voltage command of the stimulator at the top left). We noticed that, in some cells, saturating stimuli evoked adaptive currents that displayed a sigmoidal time course and were thus poorly described by a double exponential (arrowhead). b Mean maximum transduction currents recorded in P1 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. cPo(X) curves plotted for P1 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. Po(X) curves were fitted with a normalised three-state Boltzmann relation: I = 1/[1 + {exp(-(X - X0)/delta0)}{1 + exp(-(X - X1)/delta1)}], where delta0 = 555 nm, delta1 = 109 nm, X0 = 213 nm and X1 = 313 nm for P1 wild-type UHCs and delta0 = 385 nm, delta1 = 108 nm, X0 = 558 nm and X1 = 229 nm for P1 Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. d Extent of adaptation curves plotted as a function of Po for P1 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. e Contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation) plotted as a function of Po for P1 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs. f Fast adaptation rates and slow adaptation rates, plotted as a function of Po for P1 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J UHCs
###end p 47

###xml 420 424 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 2938 2942 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We then studied transduction currents in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs at P7. In harmonin-b-deficient OHCs, the hair bundles are slightly misoriented, with a mean deviation of the kinocilium of 20degrees (see "Materials and methods" and Fig. 3). In addition, approximately 20% of the hair bundles are fragmented in two clumps. Notably, these anomalies are far less severe than those observed in the harmonin-null (Ush1c-/-) mice [32]. We only describe transduction currents that were recorded in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs with nonfragmented hair bundles (Fig. 3). Maximal currents were, on average, only a third of those recorded in wild-type OHCs (Fig. 5a, b; unpaired t test, p < 10-4). The averaged sensitivity of the transduction currents to hair bundle displacement was 30% lower in the mutant OHCs, with values of 3.29 +/- 0.29 mum-1 obtained for wild-type OHCs, and 2.24 +/- 0.13 mum-1 for mutant OHCs (unpaired t test, p < 10-2). In addition, X0.5 was higher in mutant  than in wild-type  OHCs (unpaired t test, p < 10-4; data not shown). We noted, however, that mutant OHCs with transduction currents larger than 450 pA displayed an average Po(X) curve that could be superimposed to that of the wild type (Fig. 5c). The mean extent of adaptation was smaller in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J than in wild-type OHCs (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4, Fig. 5d). A significant correlation was observed between the extent of adaptation and the maximal current amplitude: the extent of adaptation tended to increase with current amplitude (two-tailed Pearson's correlation test, p < 10-4). This correlation biased the average extent of adaptation in mutant cells towards the hypoadaptive behaviour. The most striking adaptation feature in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs was the considerable variability of the extent of adaptation (left and right panels of Fig. 5d). This property was not simply dictated by variations of the maximal transduction currents. Cells with similar transduction currents could indeed display very different adaptation extents, a behaviour that contrasts with that of wild-type cells (Fig. 5d left panel). In particular, among those mutant cells that displayed the largest transduction currents (Imax > 450 pA), both hyperadaptive and hypoadaptive responses were observed (Fig. 5a, middle and right panels). Fast and slow adaptation rates were lower in mutant OHCs (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4, Fig. 5e) and were not correlated with the transduction current amplitude (data not shown). In addition, the fast component of adaptation contributed a lesser fraction of the extent of adaptation in the mutant OHCs (two-way ANOVA, p < 10-4, Fig. 5e). Fig. 5Transduction current recordings in Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs. a Examples of transduction current recordings in a wild-type OHC (left panel), a hyperadaptive Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHC (middle panel) and a hypoadaptive Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHC (right panel) voltage clamped at -80 mV from P7 mice while applying different displacement steps with a glass probe in the excitatory direction and a 150-nm step in the inhibitory direction (calibrated voltage command of the stimulator at the top left). b Mean maximum transduction currents recorded in P7 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs. cPo(X) curves plotted for P7 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs with an Imax superior to 450 pA. Po(X) curves were fitted with a normalised three-state Boltzmann relation: I = 1/[1 + {exp(-(X - X0)/delta0)}{1 + exp(-(X -  X1)/delta1)}], where delta0 = 91 nm, delta1 = 31 nm, X0 = 153 nm and X1 = 64 nm for P7 wild-type OHCs and delta0 = 143 nm, delta1 = 47 nm, X0 = 162 nm and X1 = 111 nm for P7 Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs with an Imax superior to 450 pA. dLeft panel Extent of adaptation for Po = 95% plotted as a function of amplitude for each wild-type or Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHC. Correlation was estimated using Pearson's test. Middle panel Extent of adaptation curves plotted as a function of Po for P7 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs with an Imax superior to 450 pA. Right panel Variance ratios between the extent of adaptation of Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J with an Imax superior to 450 pA and wild-type OHCs plotted as a function of Po. The statistical significance for the variance ratio was estimated using the Fisher-Snedecor tables. e Contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation), fast adaptation rates and slow adaptation rates, plotted as a function of Po for P7 wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs with an Imax superior to 450 pA

###xml 2675 2679 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 3060 3064 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 3189 3193 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
We were concerned that morphological defects, albeit minimal in the hair bundles that we selected for recording (Fig. 3), could be responsible for the variations of transduction characteristics that we observed in mutant OHCs. We thus carried out a series of control experiments. First, we measured the rise time of the transduction current in wild-type and Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs. Activation kinetics of the transduction channels indeed has an influence on adaptation characteristics [50]. We did not, however, observe any statistically significant difference between mutant OHCs that hypoadapted and OHCs that hyperadapted, and also checked that activation kinetics were similar in mutant and wild-type hair cells (data not shown). This ensured that mechanical coupling of the stimulating probe was as tight with mutant as with wild-type hair bundles. In particular, slipping artefacts in the mutant would be expected to yield slower activation kinetics, which was not observed. Second, we made sure in wild-type, hypoadapting Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J or hyperadapting Ush1cdfcr-2J/dfcr-2J OHCs that the extent of adaptation as well as the I(X) curve did not depend on the exact position of the stimulating probe against the hair bundle (Supplementary Fig. 1). In particular, we did not observe a switch between hypoadapting and hyperadapting status in a given cell. Third, we tested whether our stimulation protocol was able to recruit a substantial proportion, if not all, of the stereocilia. We compared transduction currents evoked by a fluid jet, which applies a hydrodynamic force over the whole hair bundle, to those obtained with a stiff glass probe. We used P2-P3 Ush1c-/- OHCs that lack all harmonin isoforms [32] and have severely damaged hair bundles, thereby performing our control experiments in the worst-case scenario. Currents recorded in Ush1c-/- OHCs at a membrane potential of -80 mV were much smaller than those measured for Ush1c+/- controls or the wild type [30] but in the same range as those recorded with a stiff fibre (Fig. 6a, b). Incidentally, there was no sign of a resting transduction current at any membrane potential tested in Ush1c-/- OHCs (Fig. 6a). The latter observation was confirmed by the lack of FM1-43 (a styryl dye that permeates transduction channels [18]) loading in the absence of mechanical stimulation of the hair bundle (Fig. 6c). By P7, mechanoelectrical transduction currents in the Ush1c-/- OHCs (Fig. 6b) became almost nonexistent (20.5 +/- 5 pA, n = 26). Fig. 6Mechanoelectrical transduction current recordings in Ush1c-/- OHCs. a Examples of transducer current recordings in OHCs from Ush1c+/- control and Ush1c-/- P2 mice, while applying near-saturating sinusoidal fluid jet stimuli (methods as in [47]). Upper panels Current traces for different membrane potentials (-120 mV to 120 mV). Note the absence of resting transduction currents in Ush1c-/- OHCs. Lower panel Corresponding current-voltage curves. b Examples of transducer current recordings in OHCs from wild-type, Ush1c-/- P3 and Ush1c-/- P7 mice, while applying stiff fibre stimuli. c FM1-43 dye loading of hair cells in cochlear explants from wild-type and Ush1c-/- P2 mice (upper panels). Images were captured 15 min after a 10-s dip in 3 muM FM1-43. DIC images of the explants are shown in the lower panels
###end p 53

###xml 5582 5587 5488 5493 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ush1c</italic>
###xml 5587 5602 5493 5508 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup>

###xml 1209 5626 1209 5532 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="57">Simulated transduction currents in wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs and OHCs. <bold>a</bold> Simulations of transduction currents in UHCs. <italic>Top left panel</italic> Wild-type UHCs. As in experimental recordings (see &#8220;<xref rid="Sec2" ref-type="sec">Materials and methods</xref>&#8221;), each transduction current displays a peak that is followed by an adaptive decline to a plateau. In response to stimuli of small and intermediate magnitudes, the time course of adaptation is well described by a double exponential function. Our minimal model thus produced fast and slow components of adaptation, even though the ratio between fast and slow rates was only 2.5 in the simulation instead of 6 to 8 in the recordings. Intense stimuli produced adaptive currents with a sigmoidal time course that was poorly described by a double exponential (<italic>arrowhead</italic>). Here and in subsequent figures, the time course of hair bundle position <italic>X</italic> is shown above the corresponding transduction currents, and the rise time of hair bundle deflections is controlled by <italic>K</italic><sub>SP</sub> and <italic>&#955;</italic>. <italic>K</italic><sub>SP</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;6&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>K</italic><sub>GS</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.2&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.65&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>D&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;14&#160;nm, <italic>N&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;50, <italic>&#947;</italic>=0.07, <italic>&#955;</italic>=0.001&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.015&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;35&#160;pN and <italic>S&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;5. <italic>Top middle panel Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHC. Parameter values are the same as those used to describe wild-type UHCs but for 20% and 40% decreases in <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub> and the number of adaptation motors, respectively, leading to <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.52&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.009&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup> and <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;21&#160;pN. Note that decreasing the number of adaptation motors affects by the same proportion both the motor friction <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub> and the maximal motor force <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>. <italic>Top right panel</italic> Simulated <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub>(<italic>X</italic>) curves could be fitted with a two-state Boltzmann relation: <italic>I</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;1/[1&#8201;+&#8201;exp(&#8722;(<italic>X</italic>&#8201;&#8722;&#8201;<italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>)/<italic>&#948;</italic>], with <italic>&#948;</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;75&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;568.1&#160;nm for the wild type (<italic>black curve</italic>) and with <italic>&#948;</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;75&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;641.2&#160;nm for the <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHC (<italic>blue curve</italic>). <italic>Lower panels</italic> (<italic>from left to right</italic>) Extent of fast adaptation, contribution of fast adaptation to the adaptation extent (% of fast adaptation), fast and slow adaptation rates, all plotted as a function of <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub> for the wild-type UHC (<italic>black curve</italic>) and the <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHC (<italic>blue curve</italic>). Note that, in the case where most of the transduction channels are closed at rest, small stimuli elicited slowly adapting responses of little extent. <bold>b</bold> Simulations of transduction currents in OHCs. <italic>Top left panel</italic> wild-type OHC. Note that the time scale is different from for simulations of transduction currents in UHCs. <italic>K</italic><sub>SP</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;3&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>K</italic><sub>GS</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;6.5&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.75&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>D&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;33.1&#160;nm, <italic>N&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;150, <italic>&#947;&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;0.25, <italic>&#955;</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;0.002&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.001&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;114&#160;pN and <italic>S&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;4. <italic>Top middle left and right panels Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> OHC. Reducing <italic>N</italic>, and thus all parameters proportional to <italic>N</italic>, by 66% to account for smaller transduction currents in mutant mice as well as <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub> by 20% to reproduce the effect of harmonin-b absence unveiled in UHCs produces a hyperadaptive system with slightly faster adaptation (<italic>dark blue</italic>). In this case, parameters are the same as for the wild-type OHC, except <italic>K</italic><sub>GS</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;2.17&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.2&#160;pN&#160;nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, <italic>N&#8201;</italic>=&#8201;50, <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.333&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup> and <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;38&#160;pN. An additional reduction of the number of adaptation motors, and thus of <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub> and <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>, by 50% instead produces a hypoadaptive behaviour with slower adaptation (<italic>light blue</italic>). There, <italic>&#955;</italic><sub>a</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.167&#160;pN&#160;s nm<sup>&#8722;1</sup> and <italic>F</italic><sub>max</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;19&#160;pN. <italic>Top right panel</italic> Simulated <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub>(<italic>X</italic>) curves plotted for the wild-type OHC (<italic>black</italic>) and the <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> OHC (<italic>blue</italic>) could be fitted with a three-state Boltzmann relation: <inline-formula id="IEq15"><tex-math id="M19">\documentclass[12pt]{minimal} \usepackage{amsmath} \usepackage{wasysym} \usepackage{amsfonts} \usepackage{amssymb} \usepackage{amsbsy} \usepackage{mathrsfs} \usepackage{upgreek} \setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt} \begin{document}$$ I = 1/\left[ {1 + \left\{ {{ \exp }\left( { - \left( {X - {X_0}} \right)/{\delta_0}} \right)} \right\}\left\{ {1 + { \exp }\left( { - \left( {X - {X_1}} \right)/{\delta_1}} \right)} \right\}} \right] $$\end{document}</tex-math></inline-formula>, with <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;5.3&#160;nm, <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;11.4&#160;nm, <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;172.5&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;266&#160;nm for wild-type (black), with <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;4.7&#160;nm, <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;12.5&#160;nm, <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;142&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;253.9&#160;nm for the hyperadaptive case of <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> (<italic>dark blue</italic>) and with <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;9.5&#160;nm, <italic>&#948;</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;9.4&#160;nm, <italic>X</italic><sub>0</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;248.3&#160;nm and <italic>X</italic><sub>1</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;439&#160;nm for the hypoadaptive case of <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> (<italic>light blue</italic>). <italic>Lower left panels</italic> Extent of adaptation and fast adaptation rate plotted as a function of <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub> for the wild-type OHC (<italic>black</italic>) and the <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> OHC (<italic>blue</italic>). <italic>Lower right panels</italic> extent of adaptation at <italic>P</italic><sub>o</sub>&#8201;=&#8201;0.5 as a function of relative variations of <italic>K</italic><sub>ES</sub> and the number of adaptation motors for simulations of <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs (<italic>dotted line</italic>) and OHCs (<italic>black line</italic>). In each case, the origin corresponds to the parameter set of the corresponding <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> simulation (<italic>light blue</italic>)</p>
###xml 1209 5626 1209 5532 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="57">Simulated transduction currents in wild-type and <italic>Ush1c</italic><sup>dfcr-2J/dfcr-2J</sup> UHCs and OHCs. <bold>a</bold> Simulations of transduction currents in UHCs. <italic>Top left panel</italic> Wild-type UHCs.
